# Causes and Prevention of War: A Comprehensive Analysis

## Chapter 20: The Falklands War




### Subsection: 20.1a The Invasion of the Falkland Islands

The Falkland Islands, also known as the Malvinas, have been a subject of territorial dispute between Argentina and the United Kingdom since the 18th century. The islands are strategically located in the South Atlantic Ocean, and their control has been a matter of national pride for both countries. The invasion of the Falkland Islands by Argentina in 1982 was a significant event that led to the Falklands War.

#### The Argentine Invasion

On 2 April 1982, Argentine forces mounted an amphibious landing on the Falkland Islands, known as Operation Rosario. The invasion was met with a brief but fierce defense organized by the Falkland Islands' Governor Sir Rex Hunt. The garrison, consisting of 68 marines and eleven naval hydrographers, was assisted by 23 volunteers of the Falkland Islands Defence Force (FIDF). The invasion started with the landing of Lieutenant Commander Guillermo Sanchez-Sabarots' Amphibious Commandos Group, who attacked the empty Moody Brook barracks and then moved on Government House in Stanley. When the 2nd Marine Infantry Battalion with Assault Amphibious Vehicles arrived, the governor ordered a ceasefire and surrendered. The governor, his family, and the British military personnel were flown to Argentina that afternoon and later repatriated to the United Kingdom.

#### The British Response

The British had already taken action prior to the 2 April invasion. In response to events on South Georgia, on 29 March, Ministers decided to send the Royal Fleet Auxiliary (RFA) "Fort Austin" south from the Mediterranean to support HMS "Endurance", and the nuclear-powered fleet submarine from Gibraltar, with ordered south from Scotland the following day. Lord Carrington had wished to send a third submarine, but the decision was deferred due to concerns about the impact on operational commitments. Coincidentally, on 26 March, the submarine left Gibraltar and it was assumed in the press she was heading south. There has since been speculation that the effect of those reports was to panic the Argentine junta into invading the Falklands before submarines could be deployed; however, post-war research has established that the final decision to proceed was made at a junta meeting in Buenos Aires on 23 March.

The following day, during a crisis meeting headed by Prime Minister Margaret Thatcher, it was decided to send a naval task force to retake the Falklands. The task force, led by HMS "Hermes", was assembled in the South Atlantic and arrived at the Falklands on 21 April. The British forces, including the Royal Marines and the Parachute Regiment, landed on the islands on 21 and 22 April and began the process of retaking the islands.

The invasion of the Falkland Islands by Argentina and the subsequent British response marked the beginning of the Falklands War. The war lasted for 74 days and resulted in a British victory, with Argentina surrendering on 14 June 1982. The war had significant consequences for both countries, including economic sanctions, political instability, and a lasting impact on international relations.

### Subsection: 20.1b The British Response

The British response to the Argentine invasion of the Falkland Islands was swift and decisive. The British government, under Prime Minister Margaret Thatcher, recognized the importance of the Falklands to its national interests and was determined to reclaim the islands. The British response was characterized by a combination of diplomatic efforts, economic sanctions, and military action.

#### Diplomatic Efforts

The British government immediately initiated diplomatic efforts to resolve the conflict. The British Ambassador to Argentina, Anthony Cavendish, was recalled to London for consultations, and the British government issued a statement expressing its "deep concern" at the invasion and calling for a peaceful resolution of the dispute. The British government also sought support from the international community, particularly the United States and the European Community.

#### Economic Sanctions

In addition to diplomatic efforts, the British government imposed economic sanctions on Argentina. These sanctions included a ban on the export of arms and military equipment, a freeze on Argentine assets in the UK, and a ban on British companies investing in Argentina. These sanctions were intended to put pressure on the Argentine government and to demonstrate the seriousness of the British response to the invasion.

#### Military Action

The British government also authorized military action to retake the Falklands. The British task force, led by HMS "Hermes", was assembled in the South Atlantic and arrived at the Falklands on 21 April. The British forces, including the Royal Marines and the Parachute Regiment, landed on the islands on 21 and 22 April and began the process of retaking the islands. The British military action was successful, and the Falklands were reclaimed on 14 June 1982.

The British response to the Argentine invasion of the Falklands was a significant factor in the outcome of the Falklands War. It demonstrated the British government's determination to protect its national interests and its willingness to use military force when necessary. The British response also highlighted the importance of diplomatic efforts and economic sanctions in resolving conflicts.




### Subsection: 20.1b The Role of Nationalism

Nationalism played a significant role in the Falklands War. The conflict was largely a result of the clash between British and Argentine nationalisms, each vying for control of the Falkland Islands. This section will explore the role of nationalism in the Falklands War, focusing on the British and Argentine perspectives.

#### British Nationalism

The British nationalism that drove the British response to the Argentine invasion was deeply rooted in the country's history. The Falkland Islands have been under British control since 1833, and the British have considered them a part of their territory ever since. The British saw the invasion as a direct threat to their sovereignty and national pride. The British Prime Minister Margaret Thatcher famously stated, "We have no choice but to defend our territory. We cannot allow the Falkland Islands to be taken over by force." This statement encapsulates the British nationalism that drove their response to the invasion.

The British also saw the Falklands War as a test of their military might. The British Navy, in particular, saw the conflict as an opportunity to demonstrate its superiority. The British Navy's successful campaign to retake the Falkland Islands from Argentina was seen as a vindication of their military strategy and a boost to their national pride.

#### Argentine Nationalism

On the other hand, Argentine nationalism was also a driving force behind their invasion of the Falkland Islands. The Argentine government saw the Falkland Islands as a part of their historical territory, known as the Malvinas. The Argentine invasion was seen as a way to reclaim what they considered their rightful territory.

The Argentine nationalism was also fueled by a sense of injustice. The Argentine government saw the British control of the Falkland Islands as a vestige of colonialism and a symbol of British imperialism. The Argentine invasion was seen as a way to right this historical wrong and assert their national sovereignty.

#### Conclusion

In conclusion, nationalism played a significant role in the Falklands War. Both the British and Argentine perspectives were driven by their respective nationalisms, each vying for control of the Falkland Islands. The conflict ultimately ended with the British retaking the Falkland Islands, but the nationalist sentiments on both sides continue to influence the ongoing dispute over the islands.




### Subsection: 20.1c Key Battles and Outcomes

The Falklands War was a significant conflict that had far-reaching consequences for both Argentina and the United Kingdom. The war was marked by several key battles that shaped the outcome of the conflict.

#### The Battle of San Carlos

The Battle of San Carlos was a pivotal moment in the Falklands War. It was the first major engagement between British and Argentine forces after the Argentine invasion of the Falkland Islands. The battle took place on May 21, 1982, and was a decisive victory for the British.

The British forces, under the command of Major General Jeremy Moore, landed at San Carlos Water on May 21. The Argentine forces, under the command of Brigadier General Mario Benjamin Menndez, were caught off guard and were unable to mount a strong defense. The British forces quickly overran the Argentine positions, capturing key strategic points such as Two Sisters Hill and Mount Longdon.

The Battle of San Carlos was a significant victory for the British, as it allowed them to establish a foothold on the Falkland Islands and begin their campaign to retake the islands. It also demonstrated the superiority of the British forces, both in terms of military strategy and equipment.

#### The Battle of Goose Green

The Battle of Goose Green, also known as the Battle of Darwin, was another crucial engagement in the Falklands War. It took place on May 28, 1982, and was a significant victory for the British.

The British forces, under the command of Brigadier Julian Thompson, launched a surprise attack on the Argentine forces at Goose Green. The Argentine forces, under the command of Colonel Hctor Manuel Taborda, were caught off guard and were unable to mount a strong defense. The British forces quickly overran the Argentine positions, capturing key strategic points such as Darwin Hill and Mount Harriet.

The Battle of Goose Green was a significant victory for the British, as it allowed them to establish a strong presence on the Falkland Islands and begin their campaign to retake the islands. It also demonstrated the superiority of the British forces, both in terms of military strategy and equipment.

#### The Outcome of the Falklands War

The Falklands War ended on June 14, 1982, with the surrender of the Argentine forces. The British forces, under the command of Major General Jeremy Moore, had successfully retaken the Falkland Islands.

The outcome of the Falklands War was a significant victory for the British, both in terms of military and political implications. It demonstrated the superiority of the British forces and their ability to project power across the globe. It also reinforced the British sense of nationalism and pride in their military.

However, the Falklands War also had significant consequences for Argentina. The defeat in the Falklands War led to a period of political instability and economic crisis in the country. It also damaged Argentina's international reputation and strained its relations with other countries, particularly the United Kingdom.

In conclusion, the Falklands War was a significant conflict that had far-reaching consequences for both Argentina and the United Kingdom. The key battles of San Carlos and Goose Green played a crucial role in shaping the outcome of the war and had a lasting impact on both countries.





### Subsection: 20.1d The Impact on British and Argentine Politics

The Falklands War had a significant impact on the politics of both Argentina and the United Kingdom. The war not only shaped the political landscape of these countries but also had long-lasting effects on their foreign policies.

#### The Impact on Argentine Politics

The Falklands War was a major turning point in Argentine politics. The war was seen as a humiliating defeat for Argentina, and it led to a significant shift in the country's political landscape. The war exposed the weaknesses of the Argentine military and the incompetence of the ruling junta. This led to a loss of public support for the junta and ultimately led to its downfall.

The war also had a significant impact on Argentina's foreign policy. The Argentine government's decision to invade the Falkland Islands was met with strong international condemnation, particularly from the United Kingdom. This led to a strained relationship between Argentina and the international community, particularly the United States and the United Kingdom. The war also highlighted the importance of strong alliances and international support in times of conflict.

#### The Impact on British Politics

The Falklands War also had a significant impact on British politics. The war was seen as a major victory for the United Kingdom, and it boosted public support for the government. The war also highlighted the importance of a strong military and the need for a robust defense strategy. This led to increased defense spending and a renewed focus on military capabilities.

The war also had a significant impact on British foreign policy. The United Kingdom's strong response to the Argentine invasion of the Falkland Islands demonstrated its commitment to protecting its territories and its allies. This led to a strengthening of relationships with other countries, particularly the United States, and a renewed focus on international relations.

In conclusion, the Falklands War had a profound impact on the politics of both Argentina and the United Kingdom. The war not only shaped the political landscape of these countries but also had long-lasting effects on their foreign policies. The war serves as a reminder of the importance of strong alliances, international support, and robust defense strategies in times of conflict.


### Conclusion
In this chapter, we have explored the causes and consequences of the Falklands War. We have seen how the conflict was sparked by a combination of historical tensions, political maneuvering, and miscommunication. We have also examined the impact of the war on both the United Kingdom and Argentina, including the loss of life, economic consequences, and long-lasting political implications.

The Falklands War serves as a cautionary tale about the dangers of escalating conflicts and the importance of effective communication in international relations. It also highlights the need for strong diplomatic efforts and the role of third-party mediators in preventing and resolving conflicts. As we continue to face complex and ever-changing global challenges, the lessons learned from the Falklands War remain relevant and crucial in our efforts to maintain peace and stability.

## Chapter 21: The Gulf War
